      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amateurfunkstation.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Amateurfunkstation.jpg/220px-Amateurfunkstation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Amateurfunkstation.jpg/330px-Amateurfunkstation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Amateurfunkstation.jpg/440px-Amateurfunkstation.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amateurfunkstation.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         An example of an amateur radio station with four transceivers, amplifiers, and a computer for logging and for digital modes. On the wall are examples of various amateur radio awards, certificates, and a reception report card (QSL card) from a foreign amateur station.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <b>\n        Amateur radio\n       </b>\n       (also called\n       <b>\n        ham radio\n       </b>\n       ) describes the use of\n       <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">\n        radio frequency\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/Radio_spectrum" title="Radio spectrum">\n        spectrum\n       </a>\n       for purposes of\n       <a href="/wiki/Non-commercial" title="Non-commercial">\n        non-commercial\n       </a>\n       exchange of messages,\n       <a href="/wiki/Wireless" title="Wireless">\n        wireless\n       </a>\n       experimentation, self-training, private recreation,\n       <a href="/wiki/Radiosport" title="Radiosport">\n        radiosport\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Contesting" title="Contesting">\n        contesting\n       </a>\n       , and emergency communication. The term "amateur" is used to specify "a duly authorised person interested in radioelectric practice with a purely personal aim and without pecuniary interest;"\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-1">\n         [1]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       (either direct monetary or other similar reward) and to differentiate it from\n       <a href="/wiki/Commercial_broadcasting" title="Commercial broadcasting">\n        commercial broadcasting\n       </a>\n       , public safety (such as police and fire), or professional\n       <a href="/wiki/Two-way_radio" title="Two-way radio">\n        two-way radio\n       </a>\n       services (such as maritime, aviation, taxis, etc.).\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The amateur radio service (\n       <i>\n        amateur service\n       </i>\n       and\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/Amateur-satellite_service" title="Amateur-satellite service">\n         amateur-satellite service\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       ) is established by the\n       <a href="/wiki/International_Telecommunication_Union" title="International Telecommunication Union">\n        International Telecommunication Union\n       </a>\n       (ITU) through the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_Regulations" title="Radio Regulations">\n        Radio Regulations\n       </a>\n       . National governments regulate technical and operational characteristics of transmissions and issue individual stations licenses with an identifying\n       <a href="/wiki/Call_sign" title="Call sign">\n        call sign\n       </a>\n       . Prospective\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_operator" title="Amateur radio operator">\n        amateur operators\n       </a>\n       are tested for their understanding of key concepts in electronics and the host government\'s radio regulations. Radio amateurs use a variety of voice, text, image, and data communications modes and have access to frequency allocations throughout the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RF_spectrum" title="RF spectrum">\n        RF spectrum\n       </a>\n       to enable communication across a city, region, country, continent, the world, or even into space.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Amateur radio is officially represented and coordinated by the\n       <a href="/wiki/International_Amateur_Radio_Union" title="International Amateur Radio Union">\n        International Amateur Radio Union\n       </a>\n       (IARU), which is organized in three regions and has as its members the national amateur radio societies which exist in most countries. According to an estimate made in 2011 by the\n       <a href="/wiki/American_Radio_Relay_League" title="American Radio Relay League">\n        American Radio Relay League\n       </a>\n       , two million people throughout the world are regularly involved with amateur radio.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-2">\n         [2]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       About 830,000\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_station" title="Amateur radio station">\n        amateur radio stations\n       </a>\n       are located in IARU Region 2 (the Americas) followed by IARU Region 3 (South and East Asia and the Pacific Ocean) with about 750,000 stations. A significantly smaller number, about 400,000, are located in IARU Region 1 (Europe, Middle East, CIS, Africa).\n      </p>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <div class="toc" id="toc">\n       <div id="History">\n        History\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Amateur_radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/History_of_amateur_radio" title="History of amateur radio">\n        History of amateur radio\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mw0rkbshack.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="778" data-file-width="1200" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mw0rkbshack.jpg/220px-Mw0rkbshack.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mw0rkbshack.jpg/330px-Mw0rkbshack.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mw0rkbshack.jpg/440px-Mw0rkbshack.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mw0rkbshack.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         An amateur radio station in the United Kingdom. Multiple transceivers are employed for different bands and modes. Computers are used for control, datamodes, SDR and logging.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The origins of amateur radio can be traced to the late 19th century, but amateur radio as practiced today began in the early 20th century. The\n       <i>\n        First Annual Official Wireless Blue Book of the Wireless Association of America\n       </i>\n       , produced in 1909, contains a list of amateur radio stations.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bluebook1909_3-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bluebook1909-3">\n         [3]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This radio\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Callbook" title="Callbook">\n        callbook\n       </a>\n       lists\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wireless_telegraph" title="Wireless telegraph">\n        wireless telegraph\n       </a>\n       stations in Canada and the United States, including 89 amateur radio stations. As with radio in general, amateur radio was associated with various amateur experimenters and hobbyists. Amateur radio enthusiasts have significantly contributed to\n       <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">\n        science\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">\n        engineering\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Industry" title="Industry">\n        industry\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Social_services" title="Social services">\n        social services\n       </a>\n       . Research by amateur operators has founded new industries,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-4">\n         [4]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       built economies,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-5">\n         [5]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       empowered nations,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">\n        <a href="#cite_note-6">\n         [6]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and saved lives in times of emergency.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">\n        <a href="#cite_note-7">\n         [7]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARRL-whatis_8-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-ARRL-whatis-8">\n         [8]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Ham radio can also be used in the classroom to teach English, map skills, geography, math, science, and computer skills.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">\n        <a href="#cite_note-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ham_radio">\n        Ham radio\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Amateur_radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ham radio">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Etymology_of_ham_radio" title="Etymology of ham radio">\n        Etymology of ham radio\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The term "ham" was first a\n       <a href="/wiki/Pejorative" title="Pejorative">\n        pejorative\n       </a>\n       term used in professional wired telegraphy during the 19th century, to mock operators with poor Morse code sending skills ("\n       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ham-fisted" title="wiktionary:ham-fisted">\n        ham-fisted\n       </a>\n       ").\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">\n        <a href="#cite_note-10">\n         [10]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">\n        <a href="#cite_note-11">\n         [11]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">\n        <a href="#cite_note-12">\n         [12]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">\n        <a href="#cite_note-13">\n         [13]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This term continued to be used after the invention of radio and the proliferation of amateur experimentation with wireless telegraphy; among land- and sea-based professional radio operators, "ham" amateurs were considered a nuisance. The use of "ham" meaning "amateurish or unskilled" survives today in other disciplines ("ham actor").\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The amateur radio community subsequently began to reclaim the word as a label of pride,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">\n        <a href="#cite_note-14">\n         [14]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and by the mid-20th century it had lost its pejorative meaning. Although not an acronym, it is often mistakenly written as "HAM" in capital letters.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Activities_and_practices">\n        Activities and practices\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Amateur_radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Activities and practices">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       The many facets of amateur radio attract practitioners with a wide range of interests. Many amateurs begin with a fascination of radio communication and then combine other personal interests to make pursuit of the hobby rewarding. Some of the focal areas amateurs pursue include\n       <a href="/wiki/Contesting" title="Contesting">\n        radio contesting\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Radio_propagation" title="Radio propagation">\n        radio propagation\n       </a>\n       study,\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_emergency_communications" title="Amateur radio emergency communications">\n        public service communication\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_homebrew" title="Amateur radio homebrew">\n        technical experimentation\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Packet_radio" title="Packet radio">\n        computer networking\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Amateur radio operators use\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_amateur_radio_modes" title="List of amateur radio modes">\n        various modes of transmission\n       </a>\n       to communicate. The two most common modes for voice transmissions are\n       <a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulation" title="Frequency modulation">\n        frequency modulation\n       </a>\n       (FM) and\n       <a href="/wiki/Single-sideband_modulation" title="Single-sideband modulation">\n        single sideband\n       </a>\n       (SSB). FM offers high quality audio signals, while SSB is better at long distance communication when\n       <a href="/wiki/Bandwidth_(signal_processing)" title="Bandwidth (signal processing)">\n        bandwidth\n       </a>\n       is restricted.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARRLfaq_15-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-ARRLfaq-15">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Telegraphy" title="Telegraphy">\n        Radiotelegraphy\n       </a>\n       using\n       <a href="/wiki/Morse_code" title="Morse code">\n        Morse code\n       </a>\n       , also known as "CW" from "\n       <a href="/wiki/Continuous_wave" title="Continuous wave">\n        continuous wave\n       </a>\n       ", is the wireless extension of landline (wired)\n       <a href="/wiki/Telegraphy" title="Telegraphy">\n        telegraphy\n       </a>\n       developed by\n       <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Morse" title="Samuel Morse">\n        Samuel Morse\n       </a>\n       and dates to the earliest days of radio. Although computer-based (digital) modes and methods have largely replaced CW for commercial and military applications, many amateur radio operators still enjoy using the CW mode\xe2\x80\x94particularly on the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shortwave" title="Shortwave">\n        shortwave\n       </a>\n       bands and for experimental work, such as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EME_(communications)" title="EME (communications)">\n        earth-moon-earth communication\n       </a>\n       , because of its inherent\n       <a href="/wiki/Signal-to-noise_ratio" title="Signal-to-noise ratio">\n        signal-to-noise ratio\n       </a>\n       advantages. Morse, using internationally agreed message encodings such as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Q_code" title="Q code">\n        Q code\n       </a>\n       , enables communication between amateurs who speak different languages. It is also popular with\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_homebrew" title="Amateur radio homebrew">\n        homebrewers\n       </a>\n       and in particular with "QRP" or very-low-power enthusiasts, as CW-only transmitters are simpler to construct, and the human ear-brain signal processing system can pull weak CW signals out of the noise where voice signals would be totally inaudible. A similar "legacy" mode popular with home constructors is\n       <a href="/wiki/Amplitude_modulation" title="Amplitude modulation">\n        amplitude modulation\n       </a>\n       (AM), pursued by many\n       <a href="/wiki/Vintage_amateur_radio" title="Vintage amateur radio">\n        vintage amateur radio\n       </a>\n       enthusiasts and aficionados of\n       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">\n        vacuum tube\n       </a>\n       technology.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Demonstrating a proficiency in Morse code was for many years a requirement to obtain an amateur license to transmit on frequencies below 30\xc2\xa0MHz. Following changes in international regulations in 2003, countries are no longer required to demand proficiency.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fcc06-178a1_16-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-fcc06-178a1-16">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The United States\n       <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">\n        Federal Communications Commission\n       </a>\n       , for example, phased out this requirement for all license classes on 23 February 2007.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-e7-729_17-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-e7-729-17">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morsedropped1_18-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-morsedropped1-18">\n         [18]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Modern personal computers have encouraged the use of\n       <a href="/wiki/Digital_data" title="Digital data">\n        digital\n       </a>\n       modes such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Radioteletype" title="Radioteletype">\n        radioteletype\n       </a>\n       (RTTY) which previously required cumbersome mechanical equipment.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Missouri_19-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Missouri-19">\n         [19]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Hams led the development of\n       <a href="/wiki/Packet_radio" title="Packet radio">\n        packet radio\n       </a>\n       in the 1970s, which has employed protocols such as\n       <a href="/wiki/AX.25" title="AX.25">\n        AX.25\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Internet_protocol_suite" title="Internet protocol suite">\n        TCP/IP\n       </a>\n       . Specialized digital modes such as\n       <a href="/wiki/PSK31" title="PSK31">\n        PSK31\n       </a>\n       allow real-time, low-power communications on the shortwave bands.\n       <a href="/wiki/EchoLink" title="EchoLink">\n        EchoLink\n       </a>\n       using\n       <a href="/wiki/Voice_over_IP" title="Voice over IP">\n        Voice over IP\n       </a>\n       technology has enabled amateurs to communicate through local Internet-connected repeaters and radio nodes,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">\n        <a href="#cite_note-20">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       while\n       <a href="/wiki/Internet_Radio_Linking_Project" title="Internet Radio Linking Project">\n        IRLP\n       </a>\n       has allowed the linking of repeaters to provide greater coverage area. Automatic link establishment (ALE) has enabled continuous amateur radio networks to operate on the\n       <a href="/wiki/High_frequency" title="High frequency">\n        high frequency\n       </a>\n       bands with global coverage. Other modes, such as FSK441 using software such as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WSJT_(Amateur_radio_software)" title="WSJT (Amateur radio software)">\n        WSJT\n       </a>\n       , are used for weak signal modes including\n       <a href="/wiki/Meteor_burst_communications" title="Meteor burst communications">\n        meteor scatter\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EME_(communications)" title="EME (communications)">\n        moonbounce\n       </a>\n       communications.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Fast scan\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_television" title="Amateur television">\n        amateur television\n       </a>\n       has gained popularity as hobbyists adapt inexpensive consumer video electronics like camcorders and video cards in\n       <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">\n        PCs\n       </a>\n       . Because of the wide\n       <a href="/wiki/Bandwidth_(signal_processing)" title="Bandwidth (signal processing)">\n        bandwidth\n       </a>\n       and stable signals required, amateur television is typically found in the\n       <a href="/wiki/70-centimeter_band" title="70-centimeter band">\n        70\xc2\xa0cm\n       </a>\n       (420\xe2\x80\x93450\xc2\xa0MHz) frequency range, though there is also limited use on\n       <a href="/wiki/33-centimeter_band" title="33-centimeter band">\n        33\xc2\xa0cm\n       </a>\n       (902\xe2\x80\x93928\xc2\xa0MHz),\n       <a href="/wiki/23-centimeter_band" title="23-centimeter band">\n        23\xc2\xa0cm\n       </a>\n       (1240\xe2\x80\x931300\xc2\xa0MHz) and higher. These requirements also effectively limit the signal range to between 20 and 60\xc2\xa0miles (30\xe2\x80\x93100\xc2\xa0km).\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Linked\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_repeater" title="Amateur radio repeater">\n        repeater\n       </a>\n       systems, however, can allow transmissions of\n       <a href="/wiki/Very_high_frequency" title="Very high frequency">\n        VHF\n       </a>\n       and higher frequencies across hundreds of miles.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fsat_21-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-fsat-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Repeaters are usually located on heights of land or tall structures and allow operators to communicate over hundreds of miles using hand-held or mobile\n       <a href="/wiki/Transceiver" title="Transceiver">\n        transceivers\n       </a>\n       . Repeaters can also be linked together by using other\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_frequency_allocations" title="Amateur radio frequency allocations">\n        amateur radio bands\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Landline" title="Landline">\n        landline\n       </a>\n       , or the\n       <a href="/wiki/Internet_Radio_Linking_Project" title="Internet Radio Linking Project">\n        Internet\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ISS-24_Doug_Wheelock_uses_ham_radio_system_1.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="6048" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/ISS-24_Doug_Wheelock_uses_ham_radio_system_1.jpg/220px-ISS-24_Doug_Wheelock_uses_ham_radio_system_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/ISS-24_Doug_Wheelock_uses_ham_radio_system_1.jpg/330px-ISS-24_Doug_Wheelock_uses_ham_radio_system_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/ISS-24_Doug_Wheelock_uses_ham_radio_system_1.jpg/440px-ISS-24_Doug_Wheelock_uses_ham_radio_system_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ISS-24_Doug_Wheelock_uses_ham_radio_system_1.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         NASA astronaut Col.\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doug_Wheelock" title="Doug Wheelock">\n          Doug Wheelock\n         </a>\n         , KF5BOC,\n         <a href="/wiki/Expedition_24" title="Expedition 24">\n          Expedition 24\n         </a>\n         flight engineer, operates the NA1SS ham radio station in the\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zvezda_Service_Module" title="Zvezda Service Module">\n          Zvezda Service Module\n         </a>\n         of the\n         <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">\n          International Space Station\n         </a>\n         . Equipment is a\n         <a href="/wiki/Kenwood_Corporation" title="Kenwood Corporation">\n          Kenwood\n         </a>\n         TM-D700E transceiver.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_satellite" title="Amateur radio satellite">\n        Amateur radio satellites\n       </a>\n       can be accessed, some using a hand-held transceiver (\n       <a href="/wiki/Walkie-talkie" title="Walkie-talkie">\n        HT\n       </a>\n       ), even, at times, using the factory "rubber duck" antenna.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">\n        <a href="#cite_note-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Hams also use the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EME_(communications)" title="EME (communications)">\n        moon\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a href="/wiki/Aurora" title="Aurora">\n        aurora borealis\n       </a>\n       , and the ionized trails of\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteor" title="Meteor">\n        meteors\n       </a>\n       as reflectors of radio waves.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WJST_23-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-WJST-23">\n         [23]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Hams can also contact the\n       <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">\n        International Space Station\n       </a>\n       (ISS) because many\n       <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">\n        astronauts\n       </a>\n       and cosmonauts are licensed as amateur radio operators.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARISS_24-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-ARISS-24">\n         [24]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astrohams_25-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-astrohams-25">\n         [25]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Amateur radio operators use their\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_station" title="Amateur radio station">\n        amateur radio station\n       </a>\n       to make contacts with individual hams as well as participating in round table discussion groups or "rag chew sessions" on the air. Some join in regularly scheduled on-air meetings with other amateur radio operators, called "\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_net" title="Amateur radio net">\n        nets\n       </a>\n       " (as in "networks"), which are moderated by a station referred to as "Net Control".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-netcontrol_26-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-netcontrol-26">\n         [26]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Nets can allow operators to learn procedures for emergencies, be an informal round table, or cover specific interests shared by a group.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Amateur radio operators, using battery- or generator-powered equipment, often provide essential communications services when regular channels are unavailable due to natural disaster or other disruptive events.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Many amateur radio operators participate in radio contests, during which an individual or team of operators typically seek to contact and exchange information with as many other amateur radio stations as possible in a given period of time. In addition to contests, a number of\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_operating_award" title="Amateur radio operating award">\n        Amateur radio operating award\n       </a>\n       schemes exist, sometimes suffixed with "on the Air", such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Summits_on_the_Air" title="Summits on the Air">\n        Summits on the Air\n       </a>\n       , Islands on the Air,\n       <a href="/wiki/Worked_All_States" title="Worked All States">\n        Worked All States\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Jamboree_on_the_Air" title="Jamboree on the Air">\n        Jamboree on the Air\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Licensing">\n        Licensing\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Amateur_radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Licensing">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Montreal-tower-top.thumb2-crop.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="519" data-file-width="650" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Montreal-tower-top.thumb2-crop.jpg/220px-Montreal-tower-top.thumb2-crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Montreal-tower-top.thumb2-crop.jpg/330px-Montreal-tower-top.thumb2-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Montreal-tower-top.thumb2-crop.jpg/440px-Montreal-tower-top.thumb2-crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Montreal-tower-top.thumb2-crop.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         The top of a tower supporting a\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yagi-Uda_antenna" title="Yagi-Uda antenna">\n          Yagi-Uda antenna\n         </a>\n         and several wire antennas, along with a Canadian flag\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ICOM_IC-P7_dscn2510a.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="427" data-file-width="320" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/ICOM_IC-P7_dscn2510a.jpg/220px-ICOM_IC-P7_dscn2510a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/18/ICOM_IC-P7_dscn2510a.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ICOM_IC-P7_dscn2510a.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         A handheld VHF/UHF transceiver\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Radio transmission permits are closely controlled by nations\' governments because radio waves propagate beyond national boundaries, and therefore radio is of international concern. Also, radio has possible clandestine uses.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Both the requirements for and privileges granted to a licensee vary from country to country, but generally follow the international regulations and standards established by the\n       <a href="/wiki/International_Telecommunication_Union" title="International Telecommunication Union">\n        International Telecommunication Union\n       </a>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ituars_27-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-ituars-27">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/World_Administrative_Radio_Conference" title="World Administrative Radio Conference">\n        World Radio Conferences\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       All countries that license citizens to use amateur radio require operators to display knowledge and understanding of key concepts, usually by passing an exam.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">\n        <a href="#cite_note-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The licenses grant hams the privilege to operate in larger segments of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">\n        radio frequency\n       </a>\n       spectrum, with a wider variety of communication techniques, and with higher power levels relative to unlicensed personal radio services (such as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citizens%27_band_radio" title="Citizens\' band radio">\n        CB radio\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Family_Radio_Service" title="Family Radio Service">\n        FRS\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/PMR446" title="PMR446">\n        PMR446\n       </a>\n       ), which require type-approved equipment restricted in mode, range, and power.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Amateur licensing is a routine civil administrative matter in many countries. Amateurs therein must pass an examination to demonstrate technical knowledge, operating competence, and awareness of legal and regulatory requirements, in order to avoid interfering with other amateurs and other radio services. A series of exams are often available, each progressively more challenging and granting more privileges: greater frequency availability, higher power output, permitted experimentation, and, in some countries, distinctive call signs. Some countries, such as the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">\n        United Kingdom\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">\n        Australia\n       </a>\n       , have begun requiring a practical assessment in addition to the written exams in order to obtain a beginner\'s license, which they call a Foundation License.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In most countries, an operator will be assigned a\n       <a href="/wiki/Call_sign" title="Call sign">\n        call sign\n       </a>\n       with their license. In some countries, a separate "station license" is required for any station used by an amateur radio operator. Amateur radio licenses may also be granted to organizations or clubs. In some countries, hams were allowed to operate\n       <i>\n        only\n       </i>\n       club stations.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CQ.E7.8E.B0.E4.BB.A3.E9.80.9A.E4.BF.A1_29-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-CQ.E7.8E.B0.E4.BB.A3.E9.80.9A.E4.BF.A1-29">\n         [29]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       An amateur radio license is valid only in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Country" title="Country">\n        country\n       </a>\n       in which it is issued or in another country that has a reciprocal licensing agreement with the issuing country. Some countries, such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">\n        Syria\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">\n        Cuba\n       </a>\n       , restrict operation by foreigners to club stations only.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In some countries, an amateur radio license is necessary in order to purchase or possess amateur radio equipment.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">\n        <a href="#cite_note-30">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_licensing_in_the_United_States" title="Amateur radio licensing in the United States">\n        Amateur radio licensing in the United States\n       </a>\n       exemplifies the way in which some countries award different levels of amateur radio licenses based on technical knowledge: three sequential levels of licensing exams (Technician Class, General Class, and Amateur Extra Class) are currently offered, which allow operators who pass them access to larger portions of the Amateur Radio spectrum and more desirable (shorter) call signs. An exam, authorized by the Federal Communications Commission (FCC), is required for all levels of the Amateur Radio license. These exams are administered by Volunteer Examiners, accredited by the FCC-recognized Volunteer Examiner Coordinator (VEC) system. The Technician Class and General Class exams consist of 35 multiple-choice questions, drawn randomly from a pool of at least 350. To pass, 26 of the 35 questions must be answered correctly.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FCC_Amateur_Exam_31-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-FCC_Amateur_Exam-31">\n         [31]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The Extra Class exam has 50 multiple choice questions (drawn randomly from a pool of at least 500), 37 of which must be answered correctly.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FCC_Amateur_Exam_31-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-FCC_Amateur_Exam-31">\n         [31]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The tests cover regulations, customs, and technical knowledge, such as FCC provisions, operating practices, advanced electronics theory, radio equipment design, and safety. Morse Code is no longer tested in the U.S. Once the exam is passed, the FCC issues an Amateur Radio license which is valid for ten years. Studying for the exam is made easier because the entire question pools for all license classes are posted in advance. The question pools are updated every four years by the National Conference of VECs.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FCC_Amateur_Exam_31-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-FCC_Amateur_Exam-31">\n         [31]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Licensing_requirements">\n        Licensing requirements\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Amateur_radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Licensing requirements">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Prospective amateur radio operators are examined on understanding of the key concepts of electronics, radio equipment, antennas,\n       <a href="/wiki/Radio_propagation" title="Radio propagation">\n        radio propagation\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">\n        RF\n       </a>\n       safety, and the radio regulations of the government granting the license. These examinations are sets of questions typically posed in either a short answer or multiple-choice format. Examinations can be administered by\n       <a href="/wiki/Bureaucrat" title="Bureaucrat">\n        bureaucrats\n       </a>\n       , non-paid certified examiners, or previously licensed amateur radio operators.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The ease with which an individual can acquire an amateur radio license varies from country to country. In some countries, examinations may be offered only once or twice a year in the national capital and can be inordinately bureaucratic (for example in\n       <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">\n        India\n       </a>\n       ) or challenging because some amateurs must undergo difficult security approval (as in\n       <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">\n        Iran\n       </a>\n       ). Currently only\n       <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">\n        Yemen\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">\n        North Korea\n       </a>\n       do not issue amateur radio licenses to their citizens, although in both cases a limited number of foreign visitors have been permitted to obtain amateur licenses in the past decade. Some developing countries, especially those in\n       <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">\n        Africa\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">\n        Asia\n       </a>\n       , and\n       <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">\n        Latin America\n       </a>\n       , require the payment of annual license fees that can be prohibitively expensive for most of their citizens. A few small countries may not have a national licensing process and may instead require prospective amateur radio operators to take the licensing examinations of a foreign country. In countries with the largest numbers of amateur radio licensees, such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">\n        Japan\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">\n        United States\n       </a>\n       , Thailand,\n       <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">\n        Canada\n       </a>\n       , and most of the countries in\n       <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">\n        Europe\n       </a>\n       , there are frequent license examinations opportunities in major cities.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Granting a separate license to a club or organization generally requires that an individual with a current and valid amateur radio license who is in good standing with the telecommunications authority assumes responsibility for any operations conducted under the club license or club call sign. A few countries may issue special licenses to novices or beginners that do not assign the individual a call sign but instead require the newly licensed individual to operate from stations licensed to a club or organization for a period of time before a higher class of license can be acquired.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Reciprocal_licensing">\n        Reciprocal licensing\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Amateur_radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Reciprocal licensing">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Further information:\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_international_operation" title="Amateur radio international operation">\n        Amateur radio international operation\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amateur_Radio_International_Agreements.png">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2284" data-file-width="4501" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Amateur_Radio_International_Agreements.png/300px-Amateur_Radio_International_Agreements.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Amateur_Radio_International_Agreements.png/450px-Amateur_Radio_International_Agreements.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Amateur_Radio_International_Agreements.png/600px-Amateur_Radio_International_Agreements.png 2x" width="300"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amateur_Radio_International_Agreements.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <center>\n          <b>\n           Reciprocal Agreements by Country\n          </b>\n         </center>\n         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">\n          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #c4000a; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\n          </span>\n          CEPT Member Nations\n         </div>\n         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">\n          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #f2f204; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\n          </span>\n          IARP Member Nations\n         </div>\n         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">\n          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:orange; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\n          </span>\n          Members of CEPT and IARP\n         </div>\n         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">\n          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00ff00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\n          </span>\n          USA and Canada Treaty, CEPT and IARP\n         </div>\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       A reciprocal licensing agreement between two countries allows bearers of an amateur radio license in one country under certain conditions to legally operate an amateur radio station in the other country without having to obtain an amateur radio license from the country being visited, or the bearer of a valid license in one country can receive a separate license and a call sign in another country, both of which have a mutually-agreed reciprocal licensing approvals. Reciprocal licensing requirements vary from country to country. Some countries have bilateral or multilateral reciprocal operating agreements allowing hams to operate within their borders with a single set of requirements. Some countries lack reciprocal licensing systems.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       When traveling abroad, visiting amateur operators must follow the rules of the country in which they wish to operate. Some countries have reciprocal\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_international_operation" title="Amateur radio international operation">\n        international operating\n       </a>\n       agreements allowing hams from other countries to operate within their borders with just their home country license. Other host countries require that the visiting ham apply for a formal permit, or even a new host country-issued license, in advance.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The reciprocal recognition of licenses frequently not only depends on the involved licensing authorities, but also on the nationality of the bearer. As an example, in the US, foreign licenses are recognized only if the bearer does not have US citizenship and holds no US license (which may differ in terms of operating privileges and restrictions). Conversely, a US citizen may operate under reciprocal agreements in Canada, but not a non-US citizen holding a US license.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Newcomers">\n        Newcomers\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Amateur_radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Newcomers">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Many people start their involvement in amateur radio by finding a local club. Clubs often provide information about licensing, local operating practices, and technical advice. Newcomers also often study independently by purchasing books or other materials, sometimes with the help of a mentor, teacher, or friend. Established amateurs who help newcomers are often referred to as "Elmers", as coined by Rodney Newkirk, W9BRD,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">\n        <a href="#cite_note-32">\n         [32]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       within the ham community.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">\n        <a href="#cite_note-33">\n         [33]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">\n        <a href="#cite_note-34">\n         [34]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In addition, many countries have national amateur radio societies which encourage newcomers and work with government communications regulation authorities for the benefit of all radio amateurs. The oldest of these societies is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Wireless_Institute_of_Australia" title="Wireless Institute of Australia">\n        Wireless Institute of Australia\n       </a>\n       , formed in 1910; other notable societies are the\n       <a href="/wiki/Radio_Society_of_Great_Britain" title="Radio Society of Great Britain">\n        Radio Society of Great Britain\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a href="/wiki/American_Radio_Relay_League" title="American Radio Relay League">\n        American Radio Relay League\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Radio_Amateurs_of_Canada" title="Radio Amateurs of Canada">\n        Radio Amateurs of Canada\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_NGOs_Network_for_Radio_and_Communication" title="Bangladesh NGOs Network for Radio and Communication">\n        Bangladesh NGOs Network for Radio and Communication\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Association_of_Radio_Transmitters" title="New Zealand Association of Radio Transmitters">\n        New Zealand Association of Radio Transmitters\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/South_African_Radio_League" title="South African Radio League">\n        South African Radio League\n       </a>\n       . (\n       <i>\n        See\n        <a href="/wiki/Category:Amateur_radio_organizations" title="Category:Amateur radio organizations">\n         Category:Amateur radio organizations\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       )\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Call_signs">\n        Call signs\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Amateur_radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Call signs">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Further information:\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_call_signs" title="Amateur radio call signs">\n        Amateur radio call signs\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       An amateur radio operator uses a\n       <b>\n        call sign\n       </b>\n       on the air to legally identify the operator and/or station.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">\n        <a href="#cite_note-35">\n         [35]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In some countries, the call sign assigned to the station must always be used, whereas in other countries, the call sign of either the operator or the station may be used.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amateurlicences_36-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-amateurlicences-36">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In certain jurisdictions, an operator may also select a "vanity" call sign although these must also conform to the issuing government\'s allocation and structure used for Amateur Radio call signs.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanity_37-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-vanity-37">\n         [37]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Some jurisdictions require a fee to obtain such a vanity call sign; in others, such as the UK, a fee is not required and the vanity call sign may be selected when the license is applied for. The FCC in the U.S. discontinued its fee for vanity call sign applications in September 2015.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">\n        <a href="#cite_note-38">\n         [38]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Call sign structure as prescribed by the ITU consists of three parts which break down as follows, using the call sign\n       <b>\n        ZS1NAT\n       </b>\n       as an example:\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        <b>\n         ZS\n        </b>\n        \xe2\x80\x93 Shows the country from which the call sign originates and may also indicate the license class. (This call sign is licensed in South Africa.)\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <b>\n         1\n        </b>\n        \xe2\x80\x93 Gives the subdivision of the country or territory indicated in the first part (this one refers to the Western Cape).\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <b>\n         NAT\n        </b>\n        \xe2\x80\x93 The final part is unique to the holder of the license, identifying that station specifically.\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <p>\n       Many countries do not follow the ITU convention for the numeral. In the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">\n        United Kingdom\n       </a>\n       the original calls G0xxx, G2xxx, G3xxx, G4xxx, were Full (A)\xc2\xa0License holders along with the last M0xxx full call signs issued by the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_%26_Guilds" title="City &amp; Guilds">\n        City &amp; Guilds\n       </a>\n       examination authority in December 2003. Additional Full Licenses were originally granted to (B)\xc2\xa0Licensees with G1xxx, G6xxx, G7xxx, G8xxx and 1991 onward with M1xxx callsigns. The newer three-level Intermediate License holders are assigned 2E0xxx and 2E1xx, and the basic Foundation License holders are granted call signs M3xxx or M6xxx.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">\n        <a href="#cite_note-39">\n         [39]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Instead of using numbers, in the UK the second letter after the initial \xe2\x80\x98G\xe2\x80\x99 identifies the station\xe2\x80\x99s location; for example, a callsign G7OOE becomes GM7OOE when that license holder is operating a station in Scotland. Prefix "GM" is Scotland, G or GE is England (the \xe2\x80\x98E\xe2\x80\x99 may be omitted), and "GW" is Wales. More information is available from the\n       <a class="external text" href="http://www.ofcom.gov.uk" rel="nofollow">\n        UK Radio &amp; Media Licensing Authority (\n        <i>\n         Ofcom\n        </i>\n        ) website\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">\n        United States\n       </a>\n       , for non-vanity licenses, the numeral indicates the geographical district the holder resided in when the license was first issued. Prior to 1978, US hams were required to obtain a new call sign if they moved out of their geographic district.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In Canada Call signs start with VA, VE, VY, VO, and CY. Call signs starting with \'V\' end with a number after to indicate the political region; prefix CY indicates geographic islands. Prefix VA1 or VE1 is\n       <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">\n        Nova Scotia\n       </a>\n       , VA2 / VE2 is\n       <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">\n        Quebec\n       </a>\n       , VA3 / VE3 is\n       <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">\n        Ontario\n       </a>\n       , VA4 / VE4 is\n       <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">\n        Manitoba\n       </a>\n       , VA5 / VE5 is\n       <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">\n        Saskatchewan\n       </a>\n       , VA6 / VE6 is\n       <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">\n        Alberta\n       </a>\n       , VA7 / VE7 is\n       <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">\n        British Columbia\n       </a>\n       , VE8 is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">\n        Northwest Territories\n       </a>\n       , VE9 is\n       <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">\n        New Brunswick\n       </a>\n       , VY0 is\n       <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">\n        Nunavut\n       </a>\n       , VY1 is the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yukon_Territory" title="Yukon Territory">\n        Yukon Territory\n       </a>\n       , VY2 is\n       <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Island" title="Prince Edward Island">\n        Prince Edward Island\n       </a>\n       , VO1 is\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newfoundland" title="Newfoundland">\n        Newfoundland\n       </a>\n       , and VO2 is\n       <a href="/wiki/Labrador" title="Labrador">\n        Labrador\n       </a>\n       . CY is for amateurs operating from Sable and/or St. Paul\xe2\x80\x99s Island, both of which require coast guard permission to get to. CY0 is\n       <a href="/wiki/Sable_Island" title="Sable Island">\n        Sable Island\n       </a>\n       and CY9 is\n       <a href="/wiki/St._Paul_Island_(Nova_Scotia)" title="St. Paul Island (Nova Scotia)">\n        St. Paul\xe2\x80\x99s Island\n       </a>\n       . The last two or three letters of the callsigns are typically the operator\'s choice (upon completing the licensing test, the ham writes three most-preferred options). Two letter callsign suffixes require a ham to have already been licensed for 5\xc2\xa0years. Callsigns in Canada can be requested with a fee.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Also, for smaller geopolitical entities, the numeral may be part of the country identification. For example, VP2xxx is in the British West Indies, which is subdivided into VP2Exx Anguilla, VP2Mxx Montserrat, and VP2Vxx British Virgin Islands. VP5xxx is in the Turks and Caicos Islands, VP6xxx is on Pitcairn Island, VP8xxx is in the Falklands, and VP9xxx is in Bermuda.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Online\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Callbook" title="Callbook">\n        callbooks\n       </a>\n       or callsign databases can be browsed or searched to find out who holds a specific callsign.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">\n        <a href="#cite_note-40">\n         [40]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       An example of an online callbook is\n       <a href="/wiki/QRZ.COM" title="QRZ.COM">\n        QRZ.COM\n       </a>\n       . Non-exhaustive lists of famous people who hold or have held amateur radio callsigns have also been compiled and published.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">\n        <a href="#cite_note-41">\n         [41]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Many jurisdictions (but not in the UK &amp; Europe) may issue specialty\n       <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_registration_plate" title="Vehicle registration plate">\n        vehicle registration plates\n       </a>\n       to licensed amateur radio operators often in order to facilitate their movement during an emergency.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARRL-plates_42-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-ARRL-plates-42">\n         [42]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">\n        <a href="#cite_note-43">\n         [43]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The fees for application and renewal are usually less than the standard rate for specialty plates.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARRL-plates_42-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-ARRL-plates-42">\n         [42]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">\n        <a href="#cite_note-44">\n         [44]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Privileges">\n        Privileges\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Amateur_radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Privileges">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       In most administrations, unlike other RF spectrum users, radio amateurs may build or modify transmitting equipment for their own use within the amateur spectrum without the need to obtain government certification of the equipment.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">\n        <a href="#cite_note-45">\n         [45]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">\n        <a href="#cite_note-46">\n         [46]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Licensed amateurs can also use any frequency in their bands (rather than being allocated fixed frequencies or channels) and can operate medium to high-powered equipment on a wide range of frequencies\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">\n        <a href="#cite_note-47">\n         [47]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       so long as they meet certain technical parameters including occupied bandwidth, power, and prevention of\n       <a href="/wiki/Spurious_emission" title="Spurious emission">\n        spurious emission\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Radio amateurs have access to frequency allocations throughout the RF spectrum, usually allowing choice of an effective frequency for communications across a local, regional, or worldwide path. The shortwave bands, or\n       <a href="/wiki/High_frequency" title="High frequency">\n        HF\n       </a>\n       , are suitable for worldwide communication, and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Very_high_frequency" title="Very high frequency">\n        VHF\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Ultra_high_frequency" title="Ultra high frequency">\n        UHF\n       </a>\n       bands normally provide local or regional communication, while the\n       <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">\n        microwave\n       </a>\n       bands have enough space, or\n       <a href="/wiki/Bandwidth_(signal_processing)" title="Bandwidth (signal processing)">\n        bandwidth\n       </a>\n       , for amateur television transmissions and high-speed\n       <a href="/wiki/Computer_network" title="Computer network">\n        computer networks\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:International_amateur_radio_symbol.svg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="320" height="340" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/International_amateur_radio_symbol.svg/170px-International_amateur_radio_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/International_amateur_radio_symbol.svg/255px-International_amateur_radio_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/International_amateur_radio_symbol.svg/340px-International_amateur_radio_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="170"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:International_amateur_radio_symbol.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         The international symbol for amateur radio, included in the logos of many\n         <a href="/wiki/International_Amateur_Radio_Union" title="International Amateur Radio Union">\n          IARU\n         </a>\n         member societies. The diamond holds a\n         <a href="/wiki/Circuit_diagram" title="Circuit diagram">\n          circuit diagram\n         </a>\n         featuring components common to every radio: an antenna,\n         <a href="/wiki/Inductor" title="Inductor">\n          inductor\n         </a>\n         and\n         <a href="/wiki/Ground_(electricity)" title="Ground (electricity)">\n          ground\n         </a>\n         .\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       In most countries, an amateur radio license grants permission to the license holder to own, modify, and operate equipment that is not certified by a governmental regulatory agency. This encourages amateur radio operators to experiment with home-constructed or modified equipment. The use of such equipment must still satisfy national and international standards on\n       <a href="/wiki/Spurious_emission" title="Spurious emission">\n        spurious emissions\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Amateur radio operators are encouraged both by regulations and tradition of respectful use of the spectrum to use as little power as possible to accomplish the communication.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FCC_section_97.313_48-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-FCC_section_97.313-48">\n         [48]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This is to minimise interference or EMC to any other device. Although allowable\n       <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">\n        power\n       </a>\n       levels are moderate by commercial standards, they are sufficient to enable global communication. Lower license classes usually have lower power limits; for example, the lowest license class in the UK (Foundation licence) has a limit of 10\xc2\xa0W.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Power limits vary from country to country and between license classes within a country. For example, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Peak_envelope_power" title="Peak envelope power">\n        peak envelope power\n       </a>\n       limits for the highest available license classes in a few selected countries are: 2.25\n       <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">\n        kW\n       </a>\n       in\n       <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">\n        Canada\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Industry_Canada_49-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Industry_Canada-49">\n         [49]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       1.5\xc2\xa0kW in the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">\n        United States\n       </a>\n       , 1.0\xc2\xa0kW in\n       <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">\n        Belgium\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">\n        Luxembourg\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">\n        Switzerland\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">\n        New Zealand\n       </a>\n       , 750\xc2\xa0W in\n       <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">\n        Germany\n       </a>\n       , 500\xc2\xa0W in\n       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">\n        Italy\n       </a>\n       , 400\xc2\xa0W in Australia,\n       <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">\n        India\n       </a>\n       and the\n       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">\n        United Kingdom\n       </a>\n       , and 150\xc2\xa0W in\n       <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">\n        Oman\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Output power limits may also depend on the mode of transmission. In Australia, for example, 400\xc2\xa0W may be used for SSB transmissions, but FM and other modes are limited to 120\xc2\xa0W.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The point at which power output is measured may also affect transmissions. The United Kingdom measures at the point the antenna is connected to the signal feed cable, which means the radio system may transmit more than 400\xc2\xa0W to overcome signal loss in the cable; conversely, Germany measures power at the output of the final amplification stage, which results in a loss in radiated power with longer cable feeds.\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">\n          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">\n           citation needed\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Certain countries permit amateur radio licence holders to hold a Notice of Variation that allows higher power to be used than normally allowed for certain specific purposes. E.g. in the UK some amateur radio licence holders are allowed to transmit using (33\xc2\xa0dBw) 2.0\xc2\xa0kW for experiments entailing using the moon as a passive radio reflector (known as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth-Moon-Earth_communication" title="Earth-Moon-Earth communication">\n        Earth-Moon-Earth communication\n       </a>\n       ) (EME).\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Band_plans_and_frequency_allocations">\n        Band plans and frequency allocations\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Amateur_radio&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Band plans and frequency allocations">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_frequency_allocations" title="Amateur radio frequency allocations">\n        Amateur radio frequency allocations\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/International_Telecommunication_Union" title="International Telecommunication Union">\n        International Telecommunication Union\n       </a>\n       (ITU) governs the allocation of communications frequencies worldwide, with participation by each nation\'s communications regulation authority. National communications regulators have some liberty to restrict access to these\n       <a href="/wiki/Bandplan" title="Bandplan">\n        bandplan\n       </a>\n       frequencies or to award additional allocations as long as radio services in other countries do not suffer interference. In some countries, specific\n       <a href="/wiki/Types_of_radio_emissions" title="Types of radio emissions">\n        emission types\n       </a>\n       are restricted to certain parts of the radio spectrum, and in most other countries,\n       <a href="/wiki/International_Amateur_Radio_Union" title="International Amateur Radio Union">\n        International Amateur Radio Union\n       </a>\n       (IARU) member societies adopt voluntary plans to ensure the most effective use of spectrum.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In a few cases, a national telecommunication agency may also allow hams to use frequencies outside of the internationally allocated amateur radio bands. In\n       <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">\n        Trinidad and Tobago\n       </a>\n       , hams are allowed to use a repeater which is located on 148.800\xc2\xa0MHz. This repeater is used and maintained by the National Emergency Management Agency (NEMA), but may be used by radio amateurs in times of emergency or during normal times to test their capability and conduct emergency drills. This repeater can also be used by non-ham NEMA staff and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/REACT_International" title="REACT International">\n        REACT\n       </a>\n       members. In Australia and New Zealand ham operators are authorized to use one of the UHF TV channels. In the U.S., amateur radio operators providing essential communication needs in connection with the immediate safety of human life and immediate protection of property when normal communication systems are not available may use any frequency including those of other radio services such as police and fire and in cases of disaster in Alaska may use the statewide emergency frequency of 5167.5\xc2\xa0kHz with restrictions upon emissions.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FCC_section_97.401_and_97.403_50-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-FCC_section_97.401_and_97.403-50">\n         [50]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Similarly, amateurs in the United States may apply to be registered with the\n       <a href="/wiki/Military_Auxiliary_Radio_System" title="Military Auxiliary Radio System">\n        Military Auxiliary Radio System\n       </a>\n       (MARS). Once approved and trained, these amateurs also operate on US government military frequencies to provide contingency communications and morale message traffic support to the military services.\n      </p>\n      <table class="navbox wikitable collapsible collapsed" style="margin: 1em auto 1em auto; text-align: center; width: 100%;">\n       <tr style="text-align:center;">\n        <th colspan="5"
